This method is highlighted as the most information-rich class in the ontology, indicating it is a well-established and significant assay type in chemistry. The question seeks to understand the principle, mechanism, and applications of IDA, particularly how it uses an indicator and a competitive binding event to detect analytes.", 'hypothetical_answer': 'An Indicator Displacement Assay (IDA) is a versatile analytical technique used to detect and quantify specific analytes in a sample. The core principle involves a receptor (host molecule) that forms a complex with a colorimetric or fluorescent indicator. This complex exhibits a distinct optical signal (such as color or fluorescence). When the target analyte is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the receptor. If the analyte has a higher affinity for the receptor, it displaces the indicator, resulting in a measurable change in the optical signal. This change can be monitored visually or instrumentally, allowing for sensitive and selective detection of the analyte. IDAs are widely used in supramolecular chemistry, environmental monitoring, and biomedical diagnostics due to their simplicity, adaptability, and ability to be tailored for various targets.', The core principle involves a receptor (host molecule) that forms a complex with a colorimetric or fluorescent indicator. This complex exhibits a distinct optical signal (such as color or fluorescence). When the target analyte is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the receptor. If the analyte has a higher affinity for the receptor, it displaces the indicator, resulting in a measurable change in the optical signal. This change can be monitored visually or instrumentally, allowing for sensitive and selective detection of the analyte. IDAs are widely used in supramolecular chemistry, environmental monitoring, and biomedical diagnostics due to their simplicity, adaptability, and ability to be tailored for various targets.', The core principle involves a receptor (host molecule) that forms a complex with a colorimetric or fluorescent indicator. This complex exhibits a distinct optical signal (such as color or fluorescence). When the target analyte is introduced, it competes with the indicator for binding to the receptor. If the analyte has a higher affinity for the receptor, it displaces the indicator, resulting in a measurable change in the optical signal. This change can be monitored visually or instrumentally, allowing for sensitive and selective detection of the analyte. IDAs are widely used in supramolecular chemistry, environmental monitoring, and biomedical diagnostics due to their simplicity, adaptability, and ability to be tailored for various targets.',
